do_single_rollout

Problem: Calculate \[\prod_{n = 1}^{13} \frac{n(n + 2)}{(n + 4)^2}.\] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Writing the product out, we get \[\frac{1 \cdot 3}{5^2} \cdot \frac{2 \cdot 4}{6^2} \cdot \frac{3 \cdot 5}{7^2} \dotsm \frac{11 \cdot 13}{15^2} \cdot \frac{12 \cdot 14}{16^2} \cdot \frac{13 \cdot 15}{17^2}.\]The two factors of 5 in the numerators cancel the two factors of 3 in the denominators. The same occurs with the two factors of 6, and so on, up to the two factors of 13. We are left with \[\frac{2 \cdot 3^2 \cdot 4^2}{14 \cdot 15 \cdot 16^2 \cdot 17^2} = \boxed{\frac{3}{161840}}.\]

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

do_single_rollout

Problem: The diagonals of a rhombus are 6 inches and 8 inches. What is the perimeter, in inches, of the rhombus below? [asy] import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); pair A = (0,0),B = (8,6),C = (16,0),D = (8,-6), E = (8,0); draw(A--B--C--D--cycle); draw(A--C,StickIntervalMarker(2,2,10)); draw(B--D,StickIntervalMarker(2,1,10)); draw(rightanglemark(A,E,B,30)); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{24}

Reference Answer: The rhombus is split into four congruent right triangles. Each right triangle has a leg of $\frac{6}{2}=3$ inches and another leg of $\frac82=4$ inches. We use the Pythagorean Theorem to solve for the length of the hypotenuse, or we recognize that $3$ and $4$ are part of the Pythagorean triple $(3,4,5)$, so the length of the hypotenuse is $5$ inches. Each side of the rhombus is $5$ inches long, so the perimeter is $4(5)=\boxed{20}$ inches.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

[asy] import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); pair A = (0,0),B = (8,6),C = (16,0),D = (8,-6), E = (8,0); draw(A--B--C--D--cycle); draw(A--C,StickIntervalMarker(2,2,10)); draw(B--D,StickIntervalMarker(2,1,10)); draw(rightanglemark(A,E,B,30)); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{24}

Reference Answer: The rhombus is split into four congruent right triangles. Each right triangle has a leg of $\frac{6}{2}=3$ inches and another leg of $\frac82=4$ inches. We use the Pythagorean Theorem to solve for the length of the hypotenuse, or we recognize that $3$ and $4$ are part of the Pythagorean triple $(3,4,5)$, so the length of the hypotenuse is $5$ inches. Each side of the rhombus is $5$ inches long, so the perimeter is $4(5)=\boxed{20}$ inches.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Let $(X, \tau)$ be a path-disconnected space. If $(X, \tau')$ is a space such that $\tau' \supset \tau$ (meaning $\tau'$ is coarser than $\tau$), is $(X, \tau')$ necessarily path-disconnected? Answer with 'Yes' or 'No' and justify your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out whether if a space X with a topology τ is path-disconnected, then any coarser topology τ' (meaning τ' is a subset of τ, so τ' has fewer open sets) would also make (X, τ') path-disconnected. Hmm. Let me start by recalling some definitions. First, a space is path-disconnected if there exist at least two points in the space that cannot be connected by a path. A path between two points x and y is a continuous function f: [0,1] → X such that f(0) = x and f(1) = y. So, path-disconnected means there's at least one pair of points with no such continuous function connecting them. Now, if we have a coarser topology τ', that means τ' has fewer open sets than τ. So, the original topology τ has more open sets, making it "finer". Since continuity of functions depends on the topologies involved, making the topology coarser could affect whether a function is continuous. Wait, if τ' is coarser, then the identity map from (X, τ) to (X, τ') is continuous. Because for any open set in τ', since τ' ⊂ τ, it's already open in τ. So the identity map id: (X, τ) → (X, τ') is continuous. Conversely, the identity map in the other direction, from (X, τ') to (X, τ), is not necessarily continuous since τ has more open sets. But how does this affect the existence of paths? Suppose in the original topology τ, there's no path between two points x and y. If we take a coarser topology τ', does that mean there might still be no path, or could there suddenly be a path? Wait, actually, if τ' is coarser, then there are fewer open sets, which could mean that more functions from [0,1] to X become continuous, because the preimage of open sets in X under τ' needs to be open in [0,1]. For example, if you make the topology on X coarser, you might have more continuous functions into X because the "target" has fewer open sets to pull back. Wait, so if τ' is coarser than τ, then a function f: [0,1] → X that is continuous with respect to τ might not be continuous with respect to τ', but a function that is continuous with respect to τ' is automatically continuous with respect to τ? Wait, no. Let's think again. If we have a function f: [0,1] → (X, τ'), then for f to be continuous, the preimage of every open set in τ' must be open in [0,1]. This demonstrates that a coarser topology can indeed make a path-disconnected space path-connected. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

Problem: Let $(X, \tau)$ be a path-disconnected space. If $(X, \tau')$ is a space such that $\tau' \supset \tau$ (meaning $\tau'$ is coarser than $\tau$), is $(X, \tau')$ necessarily path-disconnected? Answer with 'Yes' or 'No' and justify your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out whether if a space X with a topology τ is path-disconnected, then any coarser topology τ' (meaning τ' is a subset of τ, so τ' has fewer open sets) would also make (X, τ') path-disconnected. Hmm. Let me start by recalling some definitions. First, a space is path-disconnected if there exist at least two points in the space that cannot be connected by a path. A path between two points x and y is a continuous function f: [0,1] → X such that f(0) = x and f(1) = y. So, path-disconnected means there's at least one pair of points with no such continuous function connecting them. Now, if we have a coarser topology τ', that means τ' has fewer open sets than τ. So, the original topology τ has more open sets, making it "finer". Since continuity of functions depends on the topologies involved, making the topology coarser could affect whether a function is continuous. Wait, if τ' is coarser, then the identity map from (X, τ) to (X, τ') is continuous. Because for any open set in τ', since τ' ⊂ τ, it's already open in τ. So the identity map id: (X, τ) → (X, τ') is continuous. Conversely, the identity map in the other direction, from (X, τ') to (X, τ), is not necessarily continuous since τ has more open sets. But how does this affect the existence of paths? Suppose in the original topology τ, there's no path between two points x and y. If we take a coarser topology τ', does that mean there might still be no path, or could there suddenly be a path? Since τ' has fewer open sets, it's easier for a function to be continuous. For example, if you make the topology on X coarser, you might have more continuous functions into X because the "target" has fewer open sets to pull back. Wait, so if τ' is coarser than τ, then a function f: [0,1] → X that is continuous with respect to τ might not be continuous with respect to τ', but a function that is continuous with respect to τ' is automatically continuous with respect to τ? Wait, no. Let's think again. If we have a function f: [0,1] → (X, τ'), then for f to be continuous, the preimage of every open set in τ' must be open in [0,1]. If τ' is coarser than τ, then the open sets in τ' are a subset of the open sets in τ. So, if a function f is continuous into (X, τ), then it is continuous into (X, τ') as well, since the preimage of each τ'-open set is open in [0,1], because those sets are also τ-open and f is continuous into τ. Therefore, the answer is No. **Final Answer** \boxed{No} </think> To determine whether a coarser topology \(\tau'\) on a path-disconnected space \((X, \tau)\) necessarily results in a path-disconnected space \((X, \tau')\), we need to consider the impact of topology on the continuity of paths. A space is path-disconnected if there exist at least two points that cannot be connected by a continuous path. A coarser topology \(\tau'\) (with fewer open sets) can potentially allow more continuous functions from the interval \([0,1]\) to \(X\), as the preimage of open sets in \(\tau'\) must be open in \([0,1]\). Since \(\tau'\) has fewer open sets, it is easier for a function to be continuous under \(\tau'\) compared to the finer topology \(\tau\). For example, consider \(X = [0,1]\) with the discrete topology \(\tau\). In this topology, every singleton is open, making the space path-disconnected because only constant functions are continuous. If we coarsen the topology to the standard Euclidean topology \(\tau'\), the space becomes path-connected because the identity map is a continuous path. This demonstrates that a coarser topology can indeed make a path-disconnected space path-connected. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

Then, denominator 2*sqrt(tan1) ≈ 2*1.248 ≈ 2.496. So, the second term ≈ -0.948 * 3.425 / 2.496 ≈ -0.948 * 1.372 ≈ -1.301. Fourth term: same as second term but positive: ≈ 1.301. Therefore, adding second and fourth terms: -1.301 + 1.301 = 0. So that checks out as well. Therefore, the total derivative at 0 is indeed 4. That's pretty neat. So even though the function looks really complicated with all those cosines and square roots, the derivative at zero simplifies to 4 due to the structure of the terms canceling out the more complex parts. So I think the answer is 4. **Final Answer** \boxed{4} </think> To find \( g'(0) \) for the function \( g(x) = (x+1)^2 \left(1+\cos{\left(\sqrt{|\tan{(x+1)}|}\right)}\right) + \sqrt{x^4+4x+1}\left(1-\cos{\left(\sqrt{|\tan{(x+1)}|}\right)}\right) \), we start by breaking down the function into simpler components and differentiating each part. First, we denote: - \( U(x) = (x+1)^2 \) - \( V(x) = 1 + \cos\left(\sqrt{|\tan{(x+1)}|}\right) \) - \( W(x) = \sqrt{x^4 + 4x + 1} \) - \( Z(x) = 1 - \cos\left(\sqrt{|\tan{(x+1)}|}\right) \) The function \( g(x) \) can be rewritten as: \[ g(x) = U(x)V(x) + W(x)Z(x) \] Using the product rule, we find the derivative \( g'(x) \): \[ g'(x) = U'(x)V(x) + U(x)V'(x) + W'(x)Z(x) + W(x)Z'(x) \] We compute each component at \( x = 0 \): - \( U(0) = 1 \), \( U'(0) = 2 \) - \( W(0) = 1 \), \( W'(0) = 2 \) - \( V(0) = 1 + \cos(\sqrt{\tan{1}}) \), \( Z(0) = 1 - \cos(\sqrt{\tan{1}}) \) - The derivatives \( V'(0) \) and \( Z'(0) \) involve the chain rule and trigonometric derivatives, but they cancel each other out when combined. Evaluating each term at \( x = 0 \): 1. \( U'(0)V(0) = 2 \left(1 + \cos(\sqrt{\tan{1}})\right) \) 2. \( U(0)V'(0) = -\sin(\sqrt{\tan{1}}) \cdot \frac{\sec^2{1}}{2\sqrt{\tan{1}}} \) 3. \( W'(0)Z(0) = 2 \left(1 - \cos(\sqrt{\tan{1}})\right) \) 4. \( W(0)Z'(0) = \sin(\sqrt{\tan{1}}) \cdot \frac{\sec^2{1}}{2\sqrt{\tan{1}}} \) Combining these terms: - The first and third terms simplify to \( 2[1 + \cos(\sqrt{\tan{1}})] + 2[1 - \cos(\sqrt{\tan{1}})] = 4 \) - The second and fourth terms cancel each other out. Thus, the derivative \( g'(0) \) is: \[ \boxed{4} \]
